<p><b>Vertebrates</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɜːr/: 'ur' in 'fur'">ɜːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> comprise all species of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Subphylum" title="Subphylum">subphylum</a> <b>Vertebrata</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordates" title="Chordates">chordates</a> with <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">backbones</a>). Vertebrates represent the overwhelming majority of the <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordata" title="Chordata">Chordata</a>, with currently about 69,276 <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Vertebrates include the <a href="/wiki/Agnatha" title="Agnatha">jawless fish</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">jawed vertebrates</a>, which include the <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">cartilaginous fishes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batoidea" title="Batoidea">rays</a>, and ratfish) and the <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">bony fishes</a>.
</p><p>A bony <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> clade known as the <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fishes</a> is included with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods">tetrapods</a>, which are further divided into <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">Extant</a> vertebrates range in size from the <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frog</a> species <i><a href="/wiki/Paedophryne_amauensis" title="Paedophryne amauensis">Paedophryne amauensis</a></i>, at as little as 7.7 mm (0.30 in), to the <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a>, at up to 33 m (108 ft). Vertebrates make up less than five percent of all described <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_species" title="Animal species">animal species</a>; the rest are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrates" title="Invertebrates">invertebrates</a>, which lack <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral columns</a>.
</p><p>The vertebrates traditionally include the <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a>, which do not have proper <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a> due to their loss in evolution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> though their closest living relatives, the <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>, do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagfish_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagfish-6">[6]</a></sup> Hagfish do, however, possess a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cranium" title="Cranium">cranium</a>. For this reason, the vertebrate subphylum is sometimes referred to as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craniata" title="Craniata">Craniata</a>" when discussing morphology.
</p><p>Molecular analysis since 1992 has suggested that hagfish are most closely related to lampreys,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and so also are vertebrates in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> sense. Others consider them a sister group of vertebrates in the common taxon of craniata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Reproductive_systems"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Reproductive systems</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Inbreeding"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Inbreeding</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Inbreeding_avoidance"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Inbreeding avoidance</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Outcrossing"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Outcrossing</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Parthenogenesis"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Parthenogenesis</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Self-fertilization"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Self-fertilization</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>

</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word origin of <i>vertebrate</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>vertebratus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Pliny</a>), meaning <i>joint of the spine.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> language origins are still unclear.
</p><p><i>Vertebrate</i> is derived from the word <i><a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebra</a></i>, which refers to any of the bones or segments of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_column" title="Spinal column">spinal column</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_morphology">Anatomy and morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Anatomy and morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_anatomy" title="Vertebrate anatomy">Vertebrate anatomy</a></div>
<p>All vertebrates are built along the basic chordate <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">body plan</a>: a stiff rod running through the length of the animal (vertebral column and/or <a href="/wiki/Notochord" title="Notochord">notochord</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> with a hollow tube of nervous tissue (the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>) above it and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_(anatomy)" title="Gut (anatomy)">gastrointestinal tract</a> below.
</p><p>In all vertebrates, the mouth is found at, or right below, the anterior end of the animal, while the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a> opens to the exterior before the end of the body. The remaining part of the body continuing after the anus forms a <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> with vertebrae and spinal cord, but no gut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romer-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebral_column">Vertebral column</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Vertebral column">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The defining characteristic of a vertebrate is the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Notochord" title="Notochord">notochord</a> (a stiff rod of uniform composition) found in all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordata" title="Chordata">chordates</a> has been replaced by a segmented series of stiffer elements (vertebrae) separated by mobile joints (intervertebral discs, derived embryonically and evolutionarily from the notochord).
</p><p>However, a few vertebrates have secondarily lost this anatomy, retaining the notochord into adulthood, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanth</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">Jawed vertebrates</a> are typified by paired appendages (fins or legs, which may be secondarily lost), but this trait is not required in order for an animal to be a vertebrate.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="3622" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg/800px-Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg/1200px-Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg/1600px-Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Dinosaurierhalle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fossilized skeleton of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diplodocus_carnegii" title="Diplodocus carnegii">Diplodocus carnegii</a></i>, showing an extreme example of the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">backbone</a> that characterizes the vertebrates.</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gills">Gills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Gills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gills_(esox).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1847" data-file-width="2080" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Gills_%28esox%29.jpg/220px-Gills_%28esox%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Gills_%28esox%29.jpg/330px-Gills_%28esox%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Gills_%28esox%29.jpg/440px-Gills_%28esox%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gills_(esox).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gill arches bearing gills in a <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">pike</a></div></div></div>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> vertebrates breathe with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gills" title="Gills">gills</a>. The gills are carried right behind the head, bordering the posterior margins of a series of openings from the <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharynx</a> to the exterior. Each <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a> is supported by a cartilagenous or bony <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gill_arch" title="Gill arch">gill arch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a> have three pairs of arches, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartilaginous_fish" title="Cartilaginous fish">cartilaginous fish</a> have five to seven pairs, while the primitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jawless_fish" title="Jawless fish">jawless fish</a> have seven. The vertebrate ancestor no doubt had more arches than this, as some of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordata" title="Chordata">chordate</a> relatives have more than 50 pairs of gills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Romer-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibians" title="Amphibians">amphibians</a> and some primitive bony <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fishes" title="Fishes">fishes</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> bear <a href="/wiki/External_gills" title="External gills">external gills</a>, branching off from the gill arches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> These are reduced in adulthood, their function taken over by the gills proper in fishes and by <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> in most amphibians. Some amphibians retain the external larval gills in adulthood, the complex internal <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a> system as seen in fish apparently being irrevocably lost very early in the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaining_ground_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaining_ground-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the more derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a> lack gills, the gill arches form during <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">fetal development</a>, and form the basis of essential structures such as <a href="/wiki/Jaw" title="Jaw">jaws</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_gland" title="Thyroid gland">thyroid gland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a>, the <i>columella</i> (corresponding to the <a href="/wiki/Stapes" title="Stapes">stapes</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a>) and, in mammals, the <a href="/wiki/Ossicles" title="Ossicles">malleus and incus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Romer-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_nervous_system">Central nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Central nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> of vertebrates is based on a hollow <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_nerve_cord" title="Dorsal nerve cord">nerve cord</a> running along the length of the animal. Of particular importance and unique to vertebrates is the presence of <a href="/wiki/Neural_crest" title="Neural crest">neural crest</a> cells. These are progenitors of <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cells</a>, and critical to coordinating the functions of cellular components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teng2006_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teng2006-17">[17]</a></sup> Neural crest cells migrate through the body from the <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_nerve_cord" title="Dorsal nerve cord">nerve cord</a> during development, and initiate the formation of <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">neural ganglia</a> and structures such as the jaws and skull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gans1983_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gans1983-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronner2012_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bronner2012-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The vertebrates are the only <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordate</a> group to exhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalisation" title="Cephalisation">cephalisation</a>, the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> functions in the head. A slight swelling of the anterior end of the nerve cord is found in the <a href="/wiki/Lancelet" title="Lancelet">lancelet</a>, a chordate, though it lacks the eyes and other complex sense <a href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a> comparable to those of vertebrates. Other chordates do not show any trends towards cephalisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Romer-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> branches out from the nerve cord to innervate the various systems. The front end of the nerve tube is expanded by a thickening of the walls and expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_canal_of_spinal_cord" title="Central canal of spinal cord">central canal of spinal cord</a> into three primary brain vesicles: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosencephalon" title="Prosencephalon">prosencephalon</a> (forebrain), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesencephalon" title="Mesencephalon">mesencephalon</a> (midbrain) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhombencephalon" title="Rhombencephalon">rhombencephalon</a> (hindbrain), further differentiated in the various vertebrate groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hildebrand_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hildebrand-21">[21]</a></sup> Two laterally placed <a href="/wiki/Eye" title="Eye">eyes</a> form around outgrowths from the midbrain, except in <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a>, though this may be a secondary loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The forebrain is well developed and subdivided in most <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, while the midbrain dominates in many <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a>. Vesicles of the forebrain are usually paired, giving rise to hemispheres like the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemisphere" title="Cerebral hemisphere">cerebral hemispheres</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hildebrand_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hildebrand-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The resulting anatomy of the central nervous system, with a single hollow nerve cord topped by a series of (often paired) vesicles, is unique to vertebrates. All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrates" title="Invertebrates">invertebrates</a> with well-developed brains, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insects" title="Insects">insects</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiders" title="Spiders">spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a>, have a ventral rather than dorsal system of <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglions</a>, with a split <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_stem" title="Brain stem">brain stem</a> running on each side of the mouth or gut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Romer-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_fish" title="Evolution of fish">Evolution of fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_tetrapods" title="Evolution of tetrapods">Evolution of tetrapods</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_vertebrates">First vertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: First vertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="205" data-file-width="568" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg/220px-Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg/330px-Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg/440px-Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haikouichthys_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The early vertebrate <i><a href="/wiki/Haikouichthys" title="Haikouichthys">Haikouichthys</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Vertebrates originated about 525 million years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, which saw the rise in organism diversity. The earliest known vertebrate is believed to be the <i><a href="/wiki/Myllokunmingia" title="Myllokunmingia">Myllokunmingia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shu_et_al._1999_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shu_et_al._1999-1">[1]</a></sup> Another early vertebrate is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haikouichthys_ercaicunensis" title="Haikouichthys ercaicunensis">Haikouichthys ercaicunensis</a></i>. Unlike the other fauna that dominated the Cambrian, these groups had the basic vertebrate body plan: a notochord, rudimentary vertebrae, and a well-defined head and tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> All of these early vertebrates lacked <a href="/wiki/Jaw" title="Jaw">jaws</a> in the common sense and relied on filter feeding close to the seabed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> A vertebrate group of uncertain phylogeny, small-eel-like <a href="/wiki/Conodont" title="Conodont">conodonts</a>, are known from microfossils of their paired tooth segments from the late Cambrian to the end of the Triassic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_fish_to_amphibians">From fish to amphibians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: From fish to amphibians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acanthostega_BW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="302" data-file-width="800" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Acanthostega_BW.jpg/220px-Acanthostega_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Acanthostega_BW.jpg/330px-Acanthostega_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Acanthostega_BW.jpg/440px-Acanthostega_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acanthostega_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Acanthostega" title="Acanthostega">Acanthostega</a></i>, a fish-like early <a href="/wiki/Labyrinthodontia" title="Labyrinthodontia">labyrinthodont</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">jawed vertebrates</a> appeared in the latest <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a> and became common in the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a>, often known as the "Age of Fishes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica1954_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica1954-27">[27]</a></sup> The two groups of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fishes" title="Bony fishes">bony fishes</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">actinopterygii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">sarcopterygii</a>, evolved and became common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The Devonian also saw the demise of virtually all jawless fishes, save for lampreys and hagfish, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">Placodermi</a>, a group of armoured fish that dominated the entirety of that period since the late <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a>. The Devonian also saw the rise of the first <a href="/wiki/Labyrinthodontia" title="Labyrinthodontia">labyrinthodonts</a>, which was a transitional form between fishes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibians" title="Amphibians">amphibians</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesozoic_vertebrates">Mesozoic vertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mesozoic vertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amniotes" title="Amniotes">Amniotes</a> branched from labyrinthodonts in the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period. The <a href="/wiki/Parareptilia" title="Parareptilia">Parareptilia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">synapsid</a> amniotes were common during the late <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">diapsids</a> became dominant during the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a>. In the sea, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fishes" title="Bony fishes">bony fishes</a> became dominant; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a>, a derived form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinosaurs" title="Dinosaurs">dinosaurs</a>, evolved in the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The demise of the non-avian dinosaurs at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> allowed for the expansion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a>, which had evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Therapsid" title="Therapsid">therapsids</a>, a group of synapsid amniotes, during the late <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> Period.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_Mesozoic">After the Mesozoic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: After the Mesozoic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> world has seen great diversification of bony fishes, frogs, birds and mammals.
</p><p>Over half of all living vertebrate species (about 32,000 species) are fish (non-tetrapod craniates), a diverse set of lineages that inhabit all the world's aquatic ecosystems, from snow minnows (Cypriniformes) in Himalayan lakes at elevations over 4,600 metres (15,100 feet) to flatfishes (order Pleuronectiformes) in the Challenger Deep, the deepest ocean trench at about 11,000 metres (36,000 feet). Fishes of myriad varieties are the main predators in most of the world's water bodies, both freshwater and marine. The rest of the vertebrate species are tetrapods, a single lineage that includes amphibians (with roughly 7,000 species); mammals (with approximately 5,500 species); and reptiles and birds (with about 20,000 species divided evenly between the two classes). Tetrapods comprise the dominant megafauna of most terrestrial environments and also include many partially or fully aquatic groups (e.g., sea <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snakes" title="Snakes">snakes</a>, penguins, cetaceans).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are several ways of classifying animals. <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_taxonomy" title="Evolutionary taxonomy">Evolutionary systematics</a> relies on <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> history, which is determined through similarities in anatomy and, if possible, the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">Phylogenetic classification</a> is based solely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Evolutionary systematics gives an overview; phylogenetic systematics gives detail. The two systems are thus complementary rather than opposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_classification">Traditional classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Traditional classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spindle_diagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="734" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Spindle_diagram.jpg/220px-Spindle_diagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Spindle_diagram.jpg/330px-Spindle_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Spindle_diagram.jpg/440px-Spindle_diagram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spindle_diagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional spindle diagram of the evolution of the vertebrates at class level</div></div></div>
<p>Conventional classification has living vertebrates grouped into seven classes based on traditional interpretations of gross <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological</a> traits. This classification is the one most commonly encountered in school textbooks, overviews, non-specialist, and popular works. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> vertebrates are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-Romer-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Subphylum Vertebrata</b>
<ul><li>Class <a href="/wiki/Agnatha" title="Agnatha">Agnatha</a> (jawless fishes)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">Chondrichthyes</a> (cartilaginous fishes)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">Osteichthyes</a> (bony fishes)</li>
<li>Class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibia" title="Amphibia">Amphibia</a> (amphibians)</li>
<li>Class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reptilia" title="Reptilia">Reptilia</a> (reptiles)</li>
<li>Class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aves" title="Aves">Aves</a> (birds)</li>
<li>Class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammalia" title="Mammalia">Mammalia</a> (mammals)</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>In addition to these, there are two classes of extinct armoured fishes, the <a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">Placodermi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Acanthodii" title="Acanthodii">Acanthodii</a>.
</p><p>Other ways of classifying the vertebrates have been devised, particularly with emphasis on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> of <a href="/wiki/Labyrinthodontia" title="Labyrinthodontia">early amphibians</a> and reptiles. An example based on Janvier (1981, 1997), Shu <i>et al.</i> (2003), and Benton (2004)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton2004_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benton2004-33">[33]</a></sup> is given here:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Subphylum Vertebrata</b>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Palaeospondylus" title="Palaeospondylus">Palaeospondylus</a></i></li>
<li><b>Superclass <a href="/wiki/Agnatha" title="Agnatha">Agnatha</a></b> or <a href="/wiki/Cephalaspidomorphi" title="Cephalaspidomorphi">Cephalaspidomorphi</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a> and other jawless fishes)</li>
<li><b>Infraphylum <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">Gnathostomata</a></b> (vertebrates with jaws)
<ul><li>Class †<a href="/wiki/Placodermi" title="Placodermi">Placodermi</a> (extinct armoured fishes)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes">Chondrichthyes</a> (cartilaginous fishes)</li>
<li>Class †<a href="/wiki/Acanthodii" title="Acanthodii">Acanthodii</a> (extinct spiny "sharks")</li>
<li><b>Superclass <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">Osteichthyes</a></b> (bony vertebrates)
<ul><li>Class <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">Actinopterygii</a> (ray-finned bony fishes)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">Sarcopterygii</a> (lobe-finned fishes, including the tetrapods)</li>
<li>Class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibia" title="Amphibia">Amphibia</a> (amphibians, some ancestral to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amniotes" title="Amniotes">amniotes</a>)- now a paraphyletic group</li>
<li>Class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapsida" title="Synapsida">Synapsida</a> (mammals and the extinct mammal-like reptiles)</li>
<li>Class <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a> (reptiles and birds)</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>While this traditional classification is orderly, most of the groups are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a>, i.e. do not contain all descendants of the class's common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton2004_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benton2004-33">[33]</a></sup> For instance, descendants of the first reptiles include modern reptiles, as well as mammals and birds. Most of the classes listed are not "complete" (and are therefore paraphyletic) <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>, meaning they do not include all the descendants of the first representative of the group. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Agnatha" title="Agnatha">agnathans</a> have given rise to the <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">jawed vertebrates</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">bony fishes</a> have given rise to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapoda" title="Tetrapoda">land vertebrates</a>; the traditional "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labyrinthodont" title="Labyrinthodont">amphibians</a>" have given rise to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reptilia" title="Reptilia">reptiles</a> (traditionally including the <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">synapsids</a>, or mammal-like "reptiles"), which in turn have given rise to the mammals and birds. Most scientists working with vertebrates use a classification based purely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, organized by their known evolutionary history and sometimes disregarding the conventional interpretations of their anatomy and physiology.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogenetic_relationships">Phylogenetic relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Phylogenetic relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic taxonomy</a>, the relationships between animals are not typically divided into ranks, but illustrated as a nested "family tree" known as a <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a>. The one below is based on studies compiled by Philippe Janvier and others for the <i>Tree of Life Web Project</i> and Delsuc et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tol_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-tol-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_extant_species">Number of extant species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Number of extant species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The number of described vertebrate species are split evenly between <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>. The following table lists the number of described <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxa" title="Extant taxa">extant</a> species for each vertebrate <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> as estimated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List_of_Threatened_Species" title="IUCN Red List of Threatened Species">IUCN Red List of Threatened Species</a>, 2014.3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN1014_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN1014-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>

</td>
<td align="center"><b>66,178</b>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The IUCN estimates that 1,305,075 <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate#Number_of_extant_species" title="Invertebrate">extant invertebrate species</a> have been described,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN1014_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN1014-36">[36]</a></sup> which means that less than 5% of the <a href="/wiki/Animal#Number_of_extant_species" title="Animal">described animal species</a> in the world are vertebrates.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebrate_species_databases">Vertebrate species databases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Vertebrate species databases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following databases maintain (more or less) up-to-date lists of vertebrate species:
</p>
<ul><li>Fish: <a href="/wiki/FishBase" title="FishBase">Fishbase</a></li>
<li>Amphibians: <a class="external text" href="http://www.amphibiaweb.org" rel="nofollow">Amphibiaweb</a></li>
<li>Reptiles: <a href="/wiki/Reptile_Database" title="Reptile Database">Reptile Database</a></li>
<li>Birds: <a class="external text" href="https://avibase.bsc-eoc.org/avibase.jsp?lang=EN" rel="nofollow">Avibase</a></li>
<li>Mammals: <a class="external text" href="https://www.departments.bucknell.edu/biology/resources/msw3/" rel="nofollow">Mammal species of the World</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_systems">Reproductive systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reproductive systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nearly all vertebrates undergo <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>. They produce <a href="/wiki/Ploidy#Haploid_and_monoploid" title="Ploidy">haploid</a> gametes by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>. The smaller, motile gametes are <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoa</a> and the larger, non-motile gametes are <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">ova</a>. These fuse by the process of <a href="/wiki/Fertilisation" title="Fertilisation">fertilisation</a> to form diploid <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygotes</a>, which develop into new individuals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inbreeding">Inbreeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Inbreeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During sexual reproduction, mating with a close relative (<a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">inbreeding</a>) often leads to <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression">inbreeding depression</a>. Inbreeding depression is considered to be largely due to expression of deleterious <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">recessive</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19834483_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19834483-37">[37]</a></sup> The effects of inbreeding have been studied in many vertebrate species.
</p><p>In several species of fish, inbreeding was found to decrease reproductive success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16189545_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16189545-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19486236_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19486236-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23798977_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23798977-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inbreeding was observed to increase juvenile mortality in 11 small animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7043080_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7043080-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>A common breeding practice for pet dogs is mating between close relatives (e.g. between half- and full <a href="/wiki/Sibling" title="Sibling">siblings</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21737321_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21737321-42">[42]</a></sup> This practice generally has a negative effect on measures of reproductive success, including decreased litter size and puppy survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10490080_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10490080-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15803761_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15803761-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25475165_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25475165-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">Incestuous</a> matings in birds result in severe <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> costs due to inbreeding depression (e.g. reduction in hatchability of eggs and reduced progeny survival).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12144022_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12144022-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22643890_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22643890-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingma_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kingma-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inbreeding_avoidance">Inbreeding avoidance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Inbreeding avoidance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As a result of the negative fitness consequences of inbreeding, vertebrate species have evolved mechanisms to avoid inbreeding. Numerous inbreeding avoidance mechanisms operating prior to mating have been described.
</p><p>Toads and many other amphibians display <a href="/wiki/Philopatry" title="Philopatry">breeding site fidelity</a>. Individuals that return to natal ponds to breed will likely encounter <a href="/wiki/Sibling" title="Sibling">siblings</a> as potential mates. Although <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a> is possible, <i>Bufo americanus</i> siblings rarely mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waldman_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waldman-49">[49]</a></sup> These toads likely recognize and actively avoid close kins as mates. Advertisement vocalizations by males appear to serve as cues by which females recognize their kin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waldman_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Waldman-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inbreeding avoidance mechanisms can also operate subsequent to <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">copulation</a>. In guppies, a post-copulatory mechanism of inbreeding avoidance occurs based on competition between <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> of rival males for achieving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick-50">[50]</a></sup> In competitions between sperm from an unrelated male and from a full sibling male, a significant bias in paternity towards the unrelated male was observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>When female <a href="/wiki/Sand_lizard" title="Sand lizard">sand lizards</a> mate with two or more males, sperm competition within the female's reproductive tract may occur. Active selection of sperm by females appears to occur in a manner that enhances female fitness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-51">[51]</a></sup> On the basis of this selective process, the sperm of males that are more distantly related to the female are preferentially used for fertilization, rather than the sperm of close relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-51">[51]</a></sup> This preference may enhance the fitness of progeny by reducing <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression">inbreeding depression</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outcrossing">Outcrossing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Outcrossing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mating with unrelated or distantly related members of the same species is generally thought to provide the advantage of masking deleterious recessive mutations in progeny<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3898363_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3898363-52">[52]</a></sup> (and see <a href="/wiki/Heterosis" title="Heterosis">Heterosis</a>). Vertebrates have evolved numerous diverse mechanisms for avoiding close inbreeding and promoting outcrossing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21237809_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21237809-53">[53]</a></sup> (and see <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_avoidance" title="Inbreeding avoidance">Inbreeding avoidance</a>).
</p><p>Outcrossing as a way of avoiding inbreeding depression, has been especially well studied in birds. For instance, inbreeding depression occurs in the great tit when the offspring are produced as a result of a mating between close relatives. In natural populations of the <a href="/wiki/Great_tit" title="Great tit">great tit</a> (<i>Parus major</i>), inbreeding is avoided by dispersal of individuals from their birthplace, which reduces the chance of mating with a close relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18211876_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18211876-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Purple-crowned_fairywren" title="Purple-crowned fairywren">purple-crowned fairywren</a> females paired with related males may undertake <a href="/wiki/Extra-pair_copulation" title="Extra-pair copulation">extra-pair matings</a> that can reduce the negative effects of inbreeding. However, there are ecological and demographic constraints on extra pair matings. Nevertheless, 46% of broods produced by incestuously paired females contained extra-pair young.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingma_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kingma-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Southern_pied_babbler" title="Southern pied babbler">Southern pied babblers</a> (<i>Turdoides bicolor</i>) appear to avoid inbreeding in two ways. The first is through dispersal, and the second is by avoiding familiar group members as mates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22471769_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22471769-55">[55]</a></sup> Although both males and females disperse locally, they move outside the range where genetically related individuals are likely to be encountered. Within their group, individuals only acquire breeding positions when the opposite-sex breeder is unrelated.
</p><p>Cooperative breeding in birds typically occurs when offspring, usually males, delay dispersal from their natal group in order to remain with the family to help rear younger kin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26577076_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26577076-56">[56]</a></sup> Female offspring rarely stay at home, dispersing over distances that allow them to breed independently, or to join unrelated groups.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parthenogenesis">Parthenogenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Parthenogenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Parthenogenesis is a natural form of reproduction in which growth and development of embryos occur without fertilization.
</p><p>Reproduction in <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">squamate</a> reptiles is ordinarily sexual, with males having a ZZ pair of sex determining chromosomes, and females a ZW pair. However, various species, including the <a href="/wiki/Epicrates_maurus" title="Epicrates maurus">Colombian Rainbow boa</a> (<i>Epicrates maurus</i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Agkistrodon_contortrix" title="Agkistrodon contortrix">Agkistrodon contortrix</a></i> (copperhead snake) and <i><a href="/wiki/Agkistrodon_piscivorus" title="Agkistrodon piscivorus">Agkistrodon piscivorus</a></i> (cotton mouth snake) can also reproduce by facultative parthenogenesis -that is, they are capable of switching from a sexual mode of reproduction to an <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual</a> mode- resulting in production of WW female progeny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22977071_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22977071-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21868391_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21868391-58">[58]</a></sup> The WW females are likely produced by <a href="/wiki/Thelytoky#Automixis_with_terminal_fusion" title="Thelytoky">terminal automixis</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mole_salamander" title="Mole salamander">Mole salamanders</a> are an ancient (2.4-3.8 million year-old) unisexual vertebrate lineage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17546077_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17546077-59">[59]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyploid" title="Polyploid">polyploid</a> unisexual mole salamander females, a premeiotic endomitotic event doubles the number of chromosomes. As a result, the mature eggs produced subsequent to the two meiotic divisions have the same ploidy as the somatic cells of the female salamander. <a href="/wiki/Synapsis" title="Synapsis">Synapsis</a> and recombination during meiotic prophase I in these unisexual females is thought to ordinarily occur between identical sister chromosomes and occasionally between <a href="/wiki/Homologous_chromosome" title="Homologous chromosome">homologous chromosomes</a>. Thus little, if any, genetic variation is produced. Recombination between <a href="/wiki/Ploidy#Homoeologous" title="Ploidy">homeologous</a> chromosomes occurs only rarely, if at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20358399_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20358399-60">[60]</a></sup> Since production of genetic variation is weak, at best, it is unlikely to provide a benefit sufficient to account for the long-term maintenance of meiosis in these organisms. However, meiosis may have been maintained during evolution by the efficient recombinational repair of DNA damages that meiosis provides, an advantage that could be realized at each generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-fertilization">Self-fertilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vertebrate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Self-fertilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangrove_killifish" title="Mangrove killifish">mangrove killifish</a> (<i>Kryptolebias marmoratus</i>) produces both eggs and sperm by meiosis and routinely reproduces by <a href="/wiki/Reproduction#Autogamy" title="Reproduction">self-fertilisation</a>. The capacity for <a href="/wiki/Selfing" title="Selfing">selfing</a> in these fishes has apparently persisted for at least several hundred thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19706532_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19706532-62">[62]</a></sup> Each individual <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a> normally fertilizes itself when an egg and sperm that it has produced by an internal organ unite inside the fish's body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlGonadal_morphology_in_the_self-fertilizing_mangrove_killifish,_Kryptolebias_marmoratus_-_Springer_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlGonadal_morphology_in_the_self-fertilizing_mangrove_killifish,_Kryptolebias_marmoratus_-_Springer-63">[63]</a></sup> In nature, this mode of reproduction can yield highly homozygous lines composed of individuals so genetically uniform as to be, in effect, identical to one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avise_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Avise-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22990587_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22990587-65">[65]</a></sup>  Although inbreeding, especially in the extreme form of self-fertilization, is ordinarily regarded as detrimental because it leads to expression of deleterious recessive alleles, self-fertilization does provide the benefit of <i>fertilization assurance</i> (<a href="/wiki/Fertilisation#Self-Pollination" title="Fertilisation">reproductive assurance</a>) at each generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avise_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Avise-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
